3 BASELINE (SECTION C)
3.16 Cultural Heritage (terrestrial archaeology)
3.16.1 Introduction
This report reports on the results of the environmental characterization of the component
archaeological cultural heritage developed within the framework of the Environmental Impact Study of the “Project
of Mining Development for the Extraction of Clays for the Production of Rare Earth Concentrates” (in
hereinafter, the “Project”), whose Owner is REE Uno SpA (hereinafter the “Owner”), in accordance with the
provided in article 18, letter e.5) of the D.S. N°40/2012 of the Ministry of the Environment, which establishes
the Regulation of the Environmental Impact Assessment System.
The Mining Development Project of Clay Extraction for the Production of Earth Concentrate
Raras, hereinafter the Project, administratively located in the Commune of Penco and Concepción,
Province of Concepción, Biobío Region, consists of the construction and operation of a mining site
along with a processing plant for weathered granitic material, with the presence of clays. The
Regolith, hereinafter called “Clays to be processed”, is formed as a result of high weathering
from rocks and contains lanthanides adsorbed on clay minerals. Lanthanides, or Rare Earths,
There are 15 elements whose atomic number increases from Lanthanum (57) to Lutetium (71). For your
Similar chemical behavior is added to them by yttrium (Y). From now on they will be called “rare earths”.
The planned Commercial Plant has a capacity to process up to 320 tons/hour of clays and
produce approximately 3,100 tons per year of Rare Earth concentrate.
In addition to the above, the Project considers three (3) clay extraction zones (ZE Victoria Norte, ZE
Luna, ZE Maite), one (1) disposal area for waste and washed clays (ZD Neptuno), one (1) disposal area
topsoil stockpile (ZAV), a clay processing plant (the Plant) and a stockpile for the
temporary stockpile of material prior to processing, an electrical line and its connection to an existing line and
the construction of internal roads to access the different areas. Furthermore, it is considered the
implementation of temporary works, to work facilities, for the construction and enabling of the
Project works, which will be located entirely within the intervention area of the Production Plant.
Processing, so the total area of the Project is 169.19
ha.
The present study of the Archaeological Cultural Heritage component incorporated both the search for
documentary background such as reconnaissance on the ground, in order to establish the presence
or absence of resources of heritage value within the Project area.

Below are detailed the objectives of the study, the methodology used in collecting
field information and bibliographic review, as well as the results of both activities and the
general conclusions derived from your analysis.
3.16.2 Objectives
3.16.2.1 General Objective
The objective of this study is to characterize the cultural heritage component
archaeological located inside the area of influence of the Project based on a visual inspection
superficial and a background check, in order to identify the presence or absence of
archaeological elements inside and propose measures for eventual archaeological finds
depending on its location with respect to the Project works.
3.16.2.2 Specific Objectives
• Review the archaeological bibliographical background available for the Project area,
in order to determine the presence or absence of assets reported with
previously and its location with respect to the Project area.
• Carry out a visual pedestrian inspection in the Project Archeology Area of Influence,
focused on the in situ detection of finds of heritage value, as well as the detection and
evaluation of the status of records previously reported in bibliography.
• Determine the characteristics of possible archaeological finds or sites.
• Evaluate the impact that the Project works will have on the eventual records
archaeological.
• Propose measures for the adequate recording and/or treatment of possible findings, in
function of the current regulations regarding heritage.
3.16.3 Area of Influence
To define the Area of Influence of the Cultural Heritage component, the “Guide to
Environmental Impact Assessment – National Monuments belonging to the Cultural Heritage in the
SEIA” (SEA, 2012) and the “Guide on the Area of Influence in the Environmental Impact Assessment System”
(SEA, 2017), both prepared by the Environmental Evaluation Service (SEA), which allow defining
the area of influence as indicated by the D.S. No. 40/2012 (which establishes the Regulation of the System of
Environmental Assessment of the Ministry of the Environment, in force since December 24, 2013), the
which states the following in letter a) of Article 2: “…The geographical area or space, whose attributes,
natural or sociocultural elements must be considered in order to define whether the Project or
activity generates or presents any of the effects, characteristics or circumstances of article 11 of the Law,
or to justify the non-existence of said effects, characteristics or circumstances.”

actions or parts of the project that may generate some impact, direct or indirect, on the assets
archaeological”.

3.16.4 Definitions
In defining the findings, the postulates of Borrero and collaborators were taken into consideration.
(1992), for whom:
“…an “archaeological site” corresponds to a group of at least 10 elements associated
among themselves according to specialty, ergology and context; a “material concentration” refers to 2 to 10
units; and an “isolated find” corresponds to an archaeological object on the surface, without
association to any cultural element.”
Added to this classification are elements described in the Archaeological Procedure Guide (2020), such as
like:
• Immovable structure or feature, related to an intentional accumulation of stones or other
materials with various purposes that can form enclosures recognized as human construction
to inhabit permanently or temporarily or set of enclosures that involves the group
of human constructions such as village type.
• Funerary site, referring to the set of funerary contexts or structures and/or that contain remains.
human bones.
• Linear feature, related to any visible human traffic course on the terrain, such as
roads, cattle tracks, cart tracks, trails, railway lines, telegraph lines, among
others.
3.16.5 Legal Framework
The present study is framed in the provisions of the current Chilean environmental regulations, which establishes
a series of regulations relating to the protection and study of cultural heritage, applicable to any work
that entails the intervention of the soil and/or subsoil of the national territory and that could potentially
cause complete or partial damage to the natural and/or artificial elements that make up the
cultural heritage present within the Project Area of Influence (AI).
Below, the legal bodies involved in the protection of heritage are briefly summarized.
cultural.
3.16.5.1 Law No. 19,300 “On General Bases of the Environment”
This law is based on the principle of “The right to live in an environment free of pollution,
environmental protection, nature preservation and heritage conservation
environmental[…]” (Article 1), considering the “Environment” as “the global system constituted by
natural and artificial elements of a physical, chemical or biological, sociocultural nature and their
3-5
==================================================================================
PAGE 19
==================================================================================
Chapter 3 Baseline (Section C)
Heritage Culture
l
EIA Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Production of
Rare Earth Concentrate
interactions, in permanent modification by human or natural action and that governs or conditions the
existence and development of life in its multiple manifestations” (Article 2, Letter ll).
Based on this principle, it is established that, prior to generating changes in the environment, each Owner
of investment projects (listed in article 10 of this law), you must consider the preparation of
various previous studies that, according to their susceptibility to generate impacts on the
environmental components, can be presented as an “Environmental Impact Statement”, or
well, as an “Environmental Impact Study”. The first is defined as “the descriptive document of
an activity or project that is intended to be carried out, or the modifications that will be introduced, granted
under oath by the respective holder, the content of which allows the competent body to evaluate whether his
environmental impact conforms to current environmental standards” (Article 2, Letter f), while the
“Environmental Impact Study” is described as “the document that describes in detail the
characteristics of a project or activity that is intended to be carried out or its modification. must
provide sound background for the prediction, identification and interpretation of its impact
environmental and describe the action(s) that will be carried out to prevent or minimize its effects.
significantly adverse” (Article 2, Letter i).
Articles 10 and 11 of the Law establish, on the one hand, the types of projects that must be
undergo the environmental impact evaluation system and, on the other hand, those that will require the
preparation of an Environmental Impact Study, respectively.
Regarding the archaeological component, in article 11 of said Law, it is established that “the projects or
activities listed in the preceding article [article 10] will require the preparation of a study of
environmental impact, if they generate or present […] f) alteration of monuments, sites with value
anthropological, archaeological, historical and, in general, those belonging to the cultural heritage”, thus establishing
the guidelines of the projects that must enter environmental evaluation and, consequently, evaluate
the potential impacts that the execution of their works could have on the archaeological component.
On the other hand, in literal e.5) of article 18 of the D.S. N°40/2012 of the Ministry of the Environment (which
approves the Regulation of the Environmental Impact Assessment System) it is established that, among the
minimum contents that must be contemplated in the Environmental Impact Studies, the following must be considered:
“[…] natural and artificial elements that make up the historical, archaeological,
paleontological, religious and in general, those that make up the cultural heritage, including the
characterization of National Monuments”.
In addition, the provisions of the “Monument Evaluation Guide” have been taken into consideration.
National Pertaining to Cultural Heritage in the SEIA”, prepared by the Evaluation Service
Environmental in 2012 (hereinafter SEA Guide, 2012), the “Archaeological Procedure Guide”, prepared by
the National Monuments Council and published in 2020 and, finally, the Guide “Evaluation Criteria
in the SEIA: Objects of protection”, published by the SEA in January 2022.

3.16.5.2 Law No. 17,288 “On National Monuments”
In this legal framework, enacted in 1970, general guidelines on the management of heritage are established.
cultural (be it historical, anthropological, archaeological and/or paleontological), in which it is established that:
“They are national monuments and remain under the tutelage and protection of the State, the places, ruins,
constructions or objects of a historical or artistic nature; burial sites or cemeteries or other remains
of the aborigines, anthropoarchaeological, paleontological or naturally formed pieces or objects, which
exist under or on the surface of the national territory or on the underwater platform of its waters
jurisdictional and
whose conservation is of interest to history, art or science; the sanctuaries of the
nature; monuments, statues, columns, pyramids, fountains, plaques, crowns, inscriptions and, in
In general, objects that are intended to remain in a public place, with a commemorative nature.
Its guardianship and protection will be exercised through the Council of National Monuments, in the manner established.
determines this law” (Title I, Article 1).
Within these, Historical Monuments are considered: “the places, ruins, constructions and objects of
fiscal, municipal or private property that due to its quality and historical or artistic interest or its
seniority, are declared such by supreme decree, issued at the request and prior agreement of the Council."
(Title III, Article 9).
Likewise, “Historical Monuments remain under the control and supervision of the Council of
National Monuments and all conservation, repair or restoration work on them, will be subject
to your prior authorization. Objects that are part of or belong to a Historical Monument may not be
be removed without authorization from the Council, which will indicate how to proceed in each case
(...)” (Article 11).
With respect to Public Monuments, the law establishes that: “They are Public Monuments and are under
the guardianship of the Council of National Monuments, the statues, columns, fountains, pyramids, plaques,
crowns, inscriptions and, in general, all objects that were placed or will be placed to
perpetuate memory in fields, streets, squares and walks or public places” (Title IV, Article 17).
Regarding the Archaeological Monuments, it is noted:
“By the sole ministry of the law, the places are Archaeological Monuments owned by the State,
ruins, and anthro-archaeological sites and pieces that exist on or under the surface of the territory
national. For the purposes of this law, paleontological pieces and
the places where they will be found.” (Title V, Article 21).
“Any natural or legal person who, when carrying out excavations at any point in the national territory and with
any purpose, I will find ruins, sites, pieces or objects of a historical, anthropological,
archaeological or paleontological, is obliged to immediately report the discovery to the
3-7
==================================================================================
PAGE 21
==================================================================================
Chapter 3 Baseline (Section C)
Cultural Heritage
EIA Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Production of
Rare Earth Concentrate
Provincial Governor, who will order the Carabineros to be responsible for its surveillance until
the Council takes charge of it.” (Article 26).
In short, Law No. 17,288 states that all National Monuments, only by the Ministry of the
law (that is, without the need for an official decree), remain under the tutelage of the State, including the sites
archaeological, which is why all projects that are submitted for evaluation must have a
“Archaeological Inspection” that accounts for the presence or absence of archaeological sites in the area of
location of their works and the possible impact that the project could have on them (removal,
destruction, modification, deterioration, etc.).
Meanwhile, in Supreme Decree No. 484 of 1990 of the Ministry of Education, which contains the “Regulation
of Law 17,288, on Archaeological, Anthropological and Paleontological Excavations and/or Prospecting”,
It is established that: “Anthropological and paleontological archaeological surveys and/or excavations, in
public or private lands, as well as the rules that regulate the authorization of the Council of
National Monuments to carry them out and the destination of the objects or species found, will be governed by
the norms contained in Law No. 17,288 and in this regulation” (Article 1).
In its article 2, meanwhile, it is stated that “for the purposes of permits and authorizations
corresponding, it will be understood as:
a. Prospecting: The study of the surface of a locality in order to discover one or more sites
archaeological, anthropological or paleontological which may include test pits and/or
surface material collections;
b. Excavation: Any alteration or intervention of an archaeological, anthropological or
paleontological, including surface collections
cie, boreholes, excavations,
treatment of structures, conservation work, restoration and, in general, any
management that alters an archaeological, anthropological or paleontological site; and
c. Sites of special relevance: Those that will be defined by the National Monuments Council on
the basis of criteria of uniqueness, potential for scientific information and heritage value.”
Finally, article 5 of the Law indicates that: “Prospecting that includes drilling wells and/or
collections of surface material and all archaeological, anthropological and
paleontological investigations, on public or private lands, may only be carried out with prior authorization from the Council of
National Monuments, through the corresponding permits.”
3.16.5.3 Law No. 19,253 “On Indigenous Peoples”
This law maintains that: "The State recognizes that the indigenous people of Chile are the descendants of the
human groups that have existed in the national territory since pre-Columbian times, which preserve
their own ethnic and cultural manifestations, with the land being for them the main foundation of their
existence and culture" (Title I, Article 1). Therefore, the recognition, respect and protection of cultures

3.16.6 Methodology
The archaeological evaluation of projects that enter the Environmental Impact Assessment System
(SEIA) considers the provisions of Law No. 19,300, the SEIA Regulations and Supreme Decree No.
484/1990 of the Ministry of Education, which contains the “Regulations of Law No. 17,288, on
Excavations and/or Archaeological, Anthropological and Paleontological Prospecting. Additionally, to
The present study considered the provisions of the Archaeological Procedure Guide (2022:71), which
established in section I of its Annex 2 as “Minimum contents of the visual inspection report”, the
review of “prehistoric and historical archaeological antecedents of the area, based on a review of the
specialized and duly updated bibliography that includes classical and contemporary literature,
in magazines, books, reports or other, as well as the projects evaluated in the Evaluation System
Environmental and development or investment.
3.16.6.1 Bibliographic Review
The first part of the study consisted of a bibliographic analysis of the archaeological background
available for the Project area, considering the commune of Penco in particular and the coastal area of
the Northwest zone of the Biobío Region in general (Bay of Concepción), with the objective of evaluating the
characteristics of prehistory and local history, as well as its constituent elements. They were reviewed
publications of magazines and books specialized in archeology, conference articles, memoirs of
degree, archaeological reports and information presented within the framework of project Baselines
nearby submitted to environmental evaluation, available on the Evaluation Service website
Environmental (SEA). Regarding the historical period, special emphasis was given to the review of the background of the
connections between the Mapuche populations and previous cultural complexes of the pottery period, as well as
to the industrialization process developed in the area, associated with the earthenware industry, and to the change of
location of the city of Concepción, initially founded in current Penco.
Similarly, a review of the registry of archaeological sites in prioritized basins was carried out.
prepared by the Ministry of Public Works (MOP, 1995) and the list of National Monuments with
declaration, prepared by the Council of National Monuments (CMN), updated as of February 2023.
3.16.6.2 Information gathering in the field
The gathering of information on the ground was carried out through a Visual Archaeological Inspection
pedestrian area of the Area of Influence (AI) of the Project, this being understood as a visual recognition of
a surface established with the
purpose of reporting or ruling out the presence of monuments
archaeological. This methodological tool did not consider the survey of the cultural material of its
general context, but only its registration in a pre-established coordinate system (UTM projection,
Datum WGS 84, Huso 18 S) with a manual navigation system (GPS navigator) and mobile equipment with

built-in cameras for photographic recording of its location and constituent elements,
using the Timestamp® program to record the orientation and coordinates of each photograph.
Likewise, for the recognition of resources of heritage value, there was a system of “Inspection Files”.
Registry”, based on the Archaeological Heritage Registry Standards conceived for the platform
SITUS of the National Monuments Council and updated according to the fields included in the “Schedule of
registry of archaeological sites” (updated as of May 2022)2.
The gathering of information on the Project AI was carried out through three field campaigns
which extended between August 3 and 4, between August 17 and 22 and December 14, 2023 (
Table 3-2).
The planning contemplated the execution of transects separated every 20 m, as well as the registration of points
of georeferenced photographic control to account for the location conditions of sectors
not prospectable, due to the presence of dense cover vegetation and/or steep slope (over 25°).

The degree of effectiveness (the ability to identify archaeological sites) of the inspection depended on a
range of factors, among which are (Gallardo and Cornejo 1986):
• Visibility: it is determined by the characteristics and variability of the environment in the area
of study and specifies the difficulties for an observer to detect the presence of
cultural materials on or below the ground. This characteristic directly affects the degree
of difficulty and reliability of detection of archaeological materials during a survey
due to the interposition of an opaque material between the observer and the archaeological evidence,
which has the consequence that materials that may be visible in certain situations or
times of the year, they are invisible in others, a product of natural factors, such as vegetation,
sedimentation, and topography, or anthropogenic factors, such as reoccupation and current land use,
among others.
• Accessibility: relates to the degree of difficulty in accessing the study area and moving around.
within it. It is linked to the restrictions imposed on the mobility of the
spotter for auk
nce a certain place, among which are: 1) climate, 2) environment
biotic, 3) topography, 4) extent of roads and 5) land tenure (or property) patterns
private).
• Obtrusiveness: it is related to the nature of the archaeological materials (artifacts,
ecofacts and traits) and their 'sensitivity' to be discovered by a specific technique
considering its ability to stand out or contrast with the environment. Thus, in the same
visibility conditions, there are some artifacts that will stand out or contrast more with the
environment, which makes them easier to detect, while others (due to their size, tone or
contrast with respect to the environment in which they are found), will not be easily detected by
the same methodology or distance between transects.
• Abundance and grouping: these factors refer to intrinsic characteristics of the elements
archaeological, rather than the conditions of the environment in which they are inserted. Abundance indicates
frequency of sites or artifacts in the study area and is expressed by the density of sites or artifacts
artifacts (number/unit area), while clustering is the degree to which artifacts
Archaeological materials are spatially aggregated.
Both the visibility factor, accessibility and obtrusiveness, are recognized by categories,
called High, Medium, Low and Null, as detailed in Table 3-3.

The second category of factors affecting the probability of discovery of materials
archaeological is made up of factors that can be controlled by the researcher,
that is, the techniques and strategies of superficial inspection, among which are:
• Intensity: understood as the amount of effort or detail dedicated to the inspection of the area
prospected and can be measured by the separation space between members of the
field, the separation between each of the imaginary transects traveled by the researcher or
the number of people/days per unit area inspected. In this study, a
intensity of transects every 20 m between each member of the team, which were available
linear form along the different polygons of the Project works, oriented from East to
West to adapt to the topography of the terrain (abundant presence of slopes and ravines),
while two transects separated every 20 m were arranged in the linear works, oriented
according to the Project design.
• Tour technique: on this occasion, to increase the possibilities of detecting findings,
each inspection strip generated by the separation of the virtual transects was traversed
through a zigzag. Additionally, control actions were considered, such as
inspection directed at points of interest, concentric microrouting with an intensity of 5 m around
to possible findings and the recording of georeferenced photographic control points to give
taking into account general site conditions and areas with access restrictions.
3.16.7 Results
3.16.7.1 Review of Bibliographic Background
i. Geographic and environmental scenario
In geographical terms, the Project area, located in the Coastal mountain range of the Biobío region,
It is located in the central-southern macro zone of the country, which extends
ende between the Chacabuco cordon
North and the Chacao-Seno de Reloncaví channel, to the south, with the Pacific Ocean as its western limit
and to the East the Andes Mountains and the border with Argentina, administratively comprising
the regions of Valparaíso, Metropolitana, Libertador Bernardo O'Higgins, Maule, Biobío, Los Ríos and
Los Lagos (Maldonado et al., 2016).
3-12
==================================================================================
PAGE 26
==================================================================================
Chapter 3 Baseline (Section C)
Cultural Heritage
EIA Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Production of
Rare Earth Concentrate
In climatic terms, the Biobío region marks the transition between the dry temperate climates of the area
central and the rainy temperate climates that develop immediately south of the Biobío River. The area of
The project location, close to the coast in the northwest sector of the region, has a climate
humid temperate, with constant humidity with rainfall that fluctuates between 1,200 mm and 2,000
mm annually from north to south of the region. The native vegetation is this area of the region is represented
by thorns, associated with boldo, peumo and quillay, while in the southern section of the region the
predominantly sclerophyllous forest (BCN 2023).
The bibliographic review presented below will give an account of the regional archaeological scenario, with
special emphasis on the coastal area of the Biobío region.
ii. Paleoindian period or initial settlement (17,000 to 11,500 AP3)
To date, no evidence of this cultural period has been recorded in the Biobío region, so
The chronocultural framework is constructed as a sequence defined for the central-south by dates
calibrated from sites such as Monte Verde (Dillehay and Collins 1988), Alero Marifilo-1 and Pucón VI (Campbell and
Quiroz 2015), located in the region of Los Lagos and La Araucanía, respectively.
At a general level, it is understood that the human populations that inhabited southern Chile in these years
They had an expeditious and poorly formatted lithic tradition, associated with hunting extinct fauna. It is
It is necessary to point out that these technological and associative patterns were interpreted based on sites
located in valleys, such as Monte Verde. However, mountain range sites, which share dates
nearby, are associated with modern fauna, fitting with the proposed Archaic I models.
by Cornejo and collaborators for central Chile. In general terms, these Archaic I groups would not have
hunted megafauna, relying on expeditious lithic technology, without the presence of materials
diagnoses (Cornejo et al. 1998).
The Monte Verde site, near the city of Puerto Montt, corresponds to an open-air camp
on the banks of the Chinchihuapi Estero, which was occupied by hunter-gatherer groups in association with
extinct fauna, among which species such as Mastodon and Paleolama stand out (Núñez et al. 2016). In the
area called Monte Verde II (14,600 calibrated years BP), organic remains of a
elongated structure, evidence of ropes and ropes, fireplace pits covered with clay,
lithic artifacts, remains of fruits, seeds, leaves, marine and estuarine algae, with properties
nutritional and medicinal (Dillehay 2004; Dillehay et al. 2008, 2015; Núñez et al. 2016). For his part, the
Monte Verde I area would correspond to a previous occupation dated to 33,000 years BP, in which
They recovered lithic pieces, which are still under evaluation (Núñez et al. 2016).
3 Years A.P: Years before the present. Standard temporal reference in archeology and related sciences, which takes as a point of
starting or counting the date of obtaining the first radiocarbon dating, in 1949, and the preparation of the first curves of
calibration for radiocarbon dating, in 1950, which fixes the counting year for A.P. dates. (Taylos, 1985).

The Alero Marifilo-1 site corresponds to an eave located on the northern coast of Lake Calafquén, locali
dad
from Pucura, in the commune of Panguipulli. It presents repeated human occupations throughout a
sequence that goes from the Early Holocene (10,200 BP) to late pottery moments (1,400 AD).
The settlement corresponds to a shell within an eave with sediments of volcanic origin. The
Earliest occupation of Marifilo-1 would account for a small group that settled for a time
short on the forward wing, surely in a phase of exploration and exploitation of new resources. Thus, for
For example, the use of basalt from the eaves as raw material to make tools and the total
absence of formatted lithic artifacts, together with the exploitation of small dams, would be giving
account of expeditious and opportunistic behavior in space and technological development (Mera and García
2004). For later stages of occupation of the eaves, the material record presents a greater density
and diversity, including artifacts and lithic waste, remains of osteofauna and manufactured instruments
from them, ceramic fragments, malacological beads, among others, highlighting the discovery of
bioanthropological remains of an infant with dates close to 5,940 ± 40 BP (Adán et al. 2004; Mera and
García 2004).
Likewise, it is necessary to highlight the presence of paleontological finds in the area,
corresponding to extinct finipleistocene fauna. Such is the case of the Pilauco site (13,220±40 AP to
10,950±50 years BP), located in the city of Osorno, where remains of extinct fauna have been recorded
(gomphotheres) and possible cultural evidence associated with unifacial stone carving debris in materials
raw materials such as basalt, quartzite and dacitic obsidian. Likewise, it should be noted that so far no
It has been possible to identify anthropogenic footprints or marks on the osteofaunistic remains analyzed (Pino,
et al. 2013; Labarca et al. 2014). In any case, it is also possible to recognize paleontological evidence
similar in the towns of Nochaco, Mulpulmo, Río Bueno, Tramalhue or Tralmahue (San Pablo) and
Huilma, in which remains of a mastodon (Cuvieronius hyodon) have even been recorded (Lagos and
Hernandez 2006).
iii. Archaic period (11,500 to 1,550)
This cultural period is the most extensive within regional prehistory and has been subdivided into three
moments: Early Archaic (11,500-8,000 cal BP), Middle Archaic (8,000-3,950 cal BP) and Late Archaic
(3,950-1,550 cal AP).
In general terms, the Archaic period begins at the end of the Pleistocene, a time characterized by
a change in climatic conditions, with an increase in temperatures, added to the extinction of
megafauna and the consequent adaptations of the populations to the environment, observing groups of
hunter gatherers with high mobility.
a) Early Archaic (11,500-8,000 cal BP)
This period constitutes an archaeological problem in terms of classification, since it represents
economic, technological and social continuities with respect to the previous period. However, it
3-14
==================================================================================
PAGE 28
==================================================================================
Chapter 3 Baseline (Section C)
Cultural Heritage
EIA Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Production of
Rare Earth Concentrate
difference due to a predominance in modern fauna hunting, marked by a climatic period
transitional period from the Pleistocene to the Holocene (Campbell and Quiroz 2015). In social terms, the density
population is low and presents great mobility, gradually occupying various spaces between the
valleys and the mountain range.
For the Early Archaic, in the coastal zone of the Biobío region, only assumptions are available, since
No settlements from that period have been identified on the coast or surrounding areas. One of the main
Reasons for this would be that the post-Pleistocene coastline, along with possible archaeological sites
associated with that period, they would have been submerged by at least 25 meters, since the sea level rose by
later periods, flooding the coast (Quiroz and Sánchez 2004).
In the pre-cordilleran-lacustrine area, on the other hand, the Marifilo-1 site stands out again, located in the
Region of La Araucanía, which presents a continuous occupation from the Paleoindian to the Formative
with a strong emphasis on collection and marked traditionality (Adan et al. 2004), in addition to sites
like A
lero Quillén (Navarro and Pino 1984) and Alero Quino (Sánchez and Inostroza 1985).
b) Middle Archaic (8,000-3,950 cal AP)
For the Middle Archaic, the population density increases, occupying and consolidating the occupation of the
coast. A technological specialization is observed aimed at the use of coastal resources,
developing harpoons, nets and hooks. In terms of lithic industry, for the Concepción area,
Coronel, Golfo de Arauco and Cañete-Lebu, the development of the “Talcahuanense” tradition is observed,
characterized by the development of bifacial projectile points with “retention barbs” (Quiroz 2001;
Quiroz and Sánchez 2004).
In the Biobío region, these occupations have been evident in the bay of Concepción, the bay of San
Vicente, Gulf of Arauco and Lebu. The location of the main sites is graphed in Figure 3-2.
archaeological sites recognized for this cultural period on the coast of the region.
The Bellavista I site, with dates of 3,870 BP and 3,330 BP (Seguel 1969) and Rocoto I and II, evidenced a
technology of projectile points without peduncle and net weights, among other instruments, associated with
ecofactual malacological remains, marine mammals and remains of coastal birds. This set accounts for
of an economy based mainly on the exploitation of coastal hunting and gathering resources.
Other similar assemblages are observed in Talcahuano 1 (4,145 ± 70 BP and 3,950 ± 60 BP) (Bustos and Vergara
1998) and Chome 1 (4,580 ± 70 AP) (Bustos and Vergara 2004).
In the insular area, the representative sites of the period are Quiriquina 1 and 2 (Seguel 1970). Both sites
They present fishing instruments similar to those recognized at the Playa Negra 9 and Bellavista I sites.
Further south, in the city of Coronel, the shell Co-3 was recorded (Quiroz, Massone and Sánchez 2001), which
presents a dating from 6330 ± 60 BP to 4,700 ± 80 BP, as well as Yobilo-1.

c) Late Archaic Period (3,950-1,550 cal BP)
This period is characterized by the early appearance of ceramics in sectors such as Purén-Lumaco and
Concepción (Campbell and Quiroz 2015). Likewise, an effective occupation of space is observed in
mountain range, valley and coastal environments. On the other hand, the maritime adaptation of hunter-gatherers
to coastal environments installs a cultural tradition, highlighting the development of technology capable of
reach places and areas far from the continent, such as Isla Mocha, Isla Quiriquina and Isla Santa María (Adan
et al. 2016).
In the central valley, the Cueva de Los Catalanes, Alero Quino 1 (1,850 AP) and Alero Quillén-1 sites present
pottery occupations on pre-ceramic components, however, no work has been developed
exhaustive about them to delve deeper into the transition from the Archaic period to the Pottery period
(Adam et al. 2016).
In mountain sectors, the sites Cabeza de Indio-1, Loncoñanco-2 and Los Resfalines-1 stand out (Adán et
al. 2016).
On the coast, a large part of the shells recorded from the Middle Holocene onwards present in their strata
higher occupations associated with the Late Archaic. The most representative sites of this period
They correspond to Le-4 and Le-1 on the mainland and P27-1 and P30-1 on Isla Mocha (Adan et al. 2016).
Site Le-4 (Morhuilla, Lebu), is located about 90 m north of Le-2. It has a diversity of
materials, among which the malacological remains of limpets (mainly Fissurella picta) stand out,
snails (especially Tegula atra) and snails (Concholepas concholepas). Among the remains of vertebrates
The skeletal remains of fish and birds stand out, plus some scarce remains of sea lions. The material
cultural is composed of net weights with an equatorial groove on ovoid pebbles and anvils,
made from sandstone boulders (Quiroz and Sánchez 2000 in Quiroz and Sánchez 2004).
The Le-1 site (700 AD), on the other hand, has evidence of mollusk exploitation from both the
rocky intertidal as well as sandy beaches. This site presents ceramics only on its superficial level,
which could indicate that it is a temporary settlement site for potter groups, who reside
permanently inside (Massone and Cárdenas 1997 ms. in Bustos 2020). At levels without
ceramics, some lithic artifacts were also recorded, among which two stone points stood out.
Fractured projectile, triangular in shape and toothed limb, plus a scraper and some edges
flattened. Both levels presented malacological remains, fish, birds and marine mammals.
As previously mentioned, during this period evidence of the occupation of Isla
Mocha, where the work carried out to date indicates that its settlement would have begun in these
moments. The Isla Mocha sites, P27-1 (3,200 AP) and P30-1 (3,500 AP), are located in sectors
coastal areas associated with fossil beaches and paleopeninsulas, on the highest marine terraces, above the
20 meters above sea level. They are single-component sites, of low stratigraphic power, generated by po
blations

hunter-gatherers of coastal adaptation, with very scarce cultural materials. The notable deficit of

Site P27-1 (3,200 B.P.) corresponds to an area of mound deposits of remains mainly

Analysis of this material has allowed us to define a hunter-gatherer strategy for these populations.
widespread broad-spectrum, with the predominant use of the rocky coast, complemented by
the use of terrestrial resources from the coastal plains and lagoons (coipos) and the interior forest (pudúes). The
Faunistic and artifactual evidence suggests that the sites correspond to temporary camps.
oriented to the exploitation of coastal resources (mollusk collection and fishing). Mammal hunting
marine and terrestrial animals (sea lions, pudúes and nutria) is very poorly documented by artifacts (without
projectile points or harpoons that directly indicate hunting) (Quiroz and Sánchez 1993).
d) Early Pottery Period (1,500-950 CAL AP)
This period is characterized by the appearance of the first great pottery tradition in the central-southern area of
Chile, called Complejo Pitrén (Aldunate 1989, Adán and Mera 1997, 2011, Campbell and Quiroz 2015).
This cultural complex is characterized by having a hunter-gatherer way of life, with a pattern of
reduced mobility compared to previous periods, while its economy is based on
horticulture. Also recognized for this complex is the consolidation of the cultural tradition of
ceramics with phytomorphic and amphiobiomorphic styles, highlighting bichrome techniques of black on red
(Adam and Mera 2011). The social organization of these human groups seems to be heterarchical,
characterized by high variability and diversity of roles, status, showing high heterogeneity,
but low inequality (Adan and Mera 2011). The first large cemeteries of the city date from this period.
region.
Several researchers postulate that the Pitrén groups would have been related to other cultures
northernmost potters in Chile, such as the Molle (Norte Chico), Bato (Central zone), Llolleo groups
(Central zone) and the trans-Andean slope, such as Candelaria (Northwest of Argentina) (Dillehay 1990 and
Aldunate 1989). Pitrén's similarities with Llolleo arise from the comparison of complete vessels,
mainly symmetrical and asymmetrical jugs, where similarities are observed in terms of practices

unconscious or the “not visible”, although they would also be observed in associations of techniques and motives
shared decorations. However, Pitrén presents more variability, unlike Llolleo, which has
with more standardized productions. For Pitrén, 18 formal ceramic types are known, which include
jugs, pots, glasses, bowls, asymmetrical jugs and complex models. A special type of ceramics
This period are the jars called pic

Chico and Lof Mahuida in Temuco (Adán et al. 2016).
Pitrén ceramics are characterized by being monochrome, with thin walls, with the use of paint on
negative, of globular shapes with zoomorphic, phytomorphic and anthropomorphic designs, highlighting the
decoration with eyes shaped like coffee beans. Human burials during this phase
They present a laterally bent position, accompanied by ceramics. The careful study of the evidence
of the cemetery sites has confirmed the assumption that they were separate settlements and
distant from the habitation sites, although a direct and spatial relationship has not yet been able to be
determined (Adan et al. 2016).
The use of textiles and metallurgy has also been documented for this period. The Villa JMC-1 site, located
in Labranza, mountain sector, corresponds to a cemetery from which 9 rings of
high purity copper, remains of an ornamental piece (Pacific oyster) as a necklace joined with a
twisted cord of alpaca fibers (Vicugna pacos). It is unknown if the production of these elements is
local or foreign, but they undoubtedly report the territorial dispersion that the Pitrén Complex achieved. These
data are reinforced by the lithic evidence of the period, which accounts for the use of rocks of origin
volcanic and siliceous both on the coast and in the mountain range, which is interpreted as the participation of
these societies in vast spheres of interaction (Adan et al. 2016).
In the mountain zone in Chile and Argentina, occupations from this period are recognized in the sites La
Tereña, Haichol Cave, Angostura Mound and Los Cipreses Alero. Other sites belonging to this
complex are the Lof Mahuida and Licanco Chico cemeteries, as well as the Pichipehuenco site, located
in the mountain range sector (Adán and Mera, 1997). On the other hand, in Curarrehue, the Rilul-1 site and in Lonquimay
The Pichipehuenco site corresponds to summer-type occupations (Adán et al. 2016).
In the area of ​​the Andean-lacustrine foothills are the sites Pitrén (Lake Calafquén), Huimpil, Lago
Ranco, Trui trui (Lake Riñihue), Pucura 1, Traitraico, Challupén Alto 1 and 2 and Lliu-Lliu-1 (Lake Calafquén)
(Aldunate1989; Mera et al. 2010 in Adán et al. 2016), Population Lago Ranco, in Lago Ranco, and Los Chilcos
(Pitrén cemetery).
In the coastal and insular area of ​​the BioBío region, the sites attributed to this period are scarce.
Specifically on Mocha Island, the first clear dating of ceramic archaeological sites
They correspond to 700 AD, defining the first agro-pottery phase of the island. The identified sites are
P10-1, P25-1, P5-1 and P21-1, with dates between 430±150 AD. and 1,240 AD. and the site of Morhuilla (1,020 ±90
AD). To the south of the Lebu River there are also the sites Huechicura-1 (Cañete), Le-38 (Quinahue),
Tranaquepe-1 and Loncotripay Cemetery in Tirúa.

e) Late Pottery Period (950-400 CAL AP)
During this period of time a second great cultural tradition appears in the central-southern area: the
El Vergel Cultural Complex. Its limits would be between the Biobío rivers (Biobío region) and the Toltén (region
of Araucanía), however, findings have been found up to the coast of Cauquenes, Maule region.
The El Vergel Complex is characterized by having the first evidence of sedentary lifestyle and agriculture
(mainly quinoa and corn), combined with less recurrent hunting and gathering practices.
Likewise, the first manifestations of monumental architecture appear, with the presence of tumuli
(Campbell and Quiroz 2015). The implementation of this consolidated agricultural economy would explain the increase
of volume in population density, as well as the appearance of the first agglutinated settlements,
which would translate in historical times into the main difficulty of European advance in this region
(Urbina et al. 2022).
One of the most representative characteristics of this cultural complex corresponds to the burials
inside funerary urns, but burials also occur in cists, direct burials in the ground.
ra and
in wooden funerary canoes (wampo or trolof) (Quiroz 2001; Bahamondes et al. 2006). in the offerings
funeral homes there are rectangular - or trapezoidal - and circular copper rings, stone pipes and
ceramics, utilitarian pots with annular grooves on the neck, monochrome symmetrical and asymmetrical jugs,
slips with black and red colors, decorated with red or black on white slip, which maintain
practically the same shape as those of the Pitrén Complex, that is, the ribbon handles arise under the lip and
They frequently have vertical protuberances (Aldunate 1989 in Quiroz 2001). The ceramic complex
Vergel also presents the hourglass motif, which is why Bahamondes et al. (2006) have raised the
arrival of Inca influences or the existence of ties with Aconcagua and Diaguita populations for this
period.
In the coastal sector where the area of ​​this Project is located, according to Bahamondes et al. (2006), it
configures an area of “mouth efficiency”, as defined by Núñez for the Norte Grande
of the country, that is, a place where terrestrial hunting and gathering resources are concentrated, coupled with a
strong emphasis on the exploitation of the sea, due to the numerous resources provided by cold waters
of the Humboldt Current (Núñez 1983: 9). This would lead one to think about the existence of an important
population contingent in this area, a fact that is confirmed by the presence of numerous sites
archaeological sites (Figure 3-4), among which the Coronel sites can be mentioned (Quiroz et al.
2005), Lenga 2, Laguna La Posada, Cosmito, Playa Negra, Cerro La U, Hualpén (Sánchez 2003, 2005),
in addition to Chiguayante (Chizelle et al. 1969), those of Isla Quiriquina (Bustos et al. 1985), as well as the sites
of San Pedro (Sánchez 2003) and Michaihue, adjacent to La Candelaria (Bahamondes et al. 2006). Also
The sites of Quiriquina (Seguel 1970; Bustos 1985), Bellavista 1, present occupations from this period.
(Seguel 1969), Quinta Virginia (Oliver 1927), Cerro la U in Talcahuano, Laguna Grande, Laguna Quiñenco,
Maule Wetland, Squadron, El Calabozo, Cerro Colo-Colo (Arauco), Tubul (1140 AD ±100), Yane,
Quidico, Cañete, Huentelolén, Santa Rosa de Lebu, Boca Lebu, Lloncao and Tirúa, among others (Bustos 2020).

f) Mapuche Culture
Archeology considers the El Vergel Cultural Complex and its variants Tirúa and Valdivia (Dillehay 1990;
Quiroz 2001) as the direct historical antecedent of the Mapuche populations. Some authors
They trace these precedents even to the Pitrén Cultural Complex, suggesting that the society
Vergel and the Pitrén towns shared the vast territory between the coast, the valleys, the lagoons and the forests.
precordillerans and, as a result of cultural fusion, the historical Mapuches emerged (Aldunate
1989). In the case of Isla Mocha, Quiroz (2001) indicates that a continuity of the populations would be observed
El Vergel/Tirua from the 12th to the 17th century, without this meaning the non-existence of changes
significant.
Returning to the theme previously outlined about the contact of populations in the Biobío region
with pre-Hispanic cultures from regions located further north (or in the southern Andean area), the La
Candelaria and Coronel-2, would present elements shared with groups of Andean roots (Aconcagua
and Diaguita), while in other respects they would have maintained a local way of life (Dillehay 2011 in
Adam et al. 2016; Bahamondes 2009). In general, the impulse taken by decorated ceramics stands out,

production of metal and lithic artifacts, the appearance of textiles, along with the incorporation of corn
and domestic camelids. But, above all, technologies and manufacturing denote the reproduction of a
Diaguita and Aconcagua aesthetics, or at least their knowledge and a dialogue with them. Therefore, according to
the aforementioned authors, the Mapuche culture would also have had an Andean cultural base and
economically attractive enough for the Incas to approach this territory.
Specifically, from archaeology, Mapuche chronology sites show a continuity of
cultural practices recorded in previous periods, such as regarding patterns of
burial, where stone cists and direct burials in the ground continue to be used and
mainly in funeral canoes. These cemeteries would be scattered throughout the region, but
The sites of Lagunillas (La Posada), Quilacoya, Gomero, Chiguayante, Puente de Lebu and
Huentelolén (Bustos 2020). For their part, in the foothills lakes of the southern sector there are
the sites of Lican Ray, Huanehue and Huitag, in Lake Calafquén, in addition to several burial sites in the lake
Ranco studied by Guillermo Franco in the 1960s (Aldunate 1989), including three
cemeteries assigned to the Late/Historic Pottery period: two associated with ceramics of the Tringlo or
Ranco and one associated with Valdivia style ceramics (Mera 2008).
Regarding pottery, commonly associated with funeral sites, an influence of the
El Vergel Complex, preserving the symmetrical and asymmetrical shapes, as well as the use of modeling and
subsections. However, in the period close to Hispanic contact, within the material repertoire of the
Mapuche culture, the appearance of new models is observed, such as cups with handles, extended plates
with wide edges and large amphorae with a lip on the neck. Likewise, symmetrical jugs
vary to more stylized shapes and, in general, an increase in the size of the pieces is observed, in addition
of some changes in the decorative patterns (black or brown slip and red paint, red paint and
black on white, etc.). A diagnostic element of post-contact ceramics is the presence on the lip,
handle or body of symmetrical pieces, inlaid with small pieces of quartz or European earthenware
forming designs such as lines, Greek crosses or Saint Andrew's (Aldunate 1989; García 2007). Others
Elements introduced are wooden, stone and ceramic pans, earrings, needles, medals and
other silver and copper ornaments, glass bead necklaces, iron tools, stirrups, spurs,
horse remains and other elements of equestrian use (García 2007).
g) Historical Period (C.A. 1500 A.D. onwards)
The ethnographic chronicles report that the Mapuche populations in the Biobío region at the time of the
contact with the Spanish they had an important agricultural and livestock development, but, above all
all, with a warrior character and a highly developed military organization. The development of studies
ethnographic, iconographic and toponymy in this regard suggests that, prior to the arrival of the Spanish,
There would have been some interaction between the Mapuche populations and the Inca empire. According to Dillehay and
Gordon, the Itata/Biobío interfluve was an informal part of the southwestern socioeconomic border of the
Tawantinsuyu, that is, a territory that did not come under the political-military domain of the empire

of the north, but it constituted a front of economic expansion and exploration with a dispersed presence of

In this sense, it is worth considering that the communication between central Chile and the province of Concepción must have
having been fluid long before
the Spanish irruption of 1550, this is if we consider the ease with which
the advance of Diego de Almagro and the subsequent explorations of Villagra in 1544 and Valdivia in 1546
They traveled the path between Mapocho and the southern territories, crossing without great difficulties
the Itata River (Zabala et al., 2021). Regarding the meeting with Mapuche populations, Pedro de Valdivia points out:
“They came extremely shamelessly, in four squads, of the most brilliant and well-rounded people.
prepared of Indians that has been seen in these parts, and rather armed with the necks of rams and
sheep and sea lion skins, raw, of infinite colors, which was extremely attractive,
and large plumes, all with helmets made of those leather, like clerics' bonnets, which do not
There is an axe, no matter how sharp it may be, that hurts whoever carries it, with a lot of arrowcraft.
and spears of twenty to twenty-five spans, and maces and clubs; “They don’t fight with stones.” Peter of
Valdivia. 1929 [1545-1552], Letters, p. 204 (Letter to Emperor Charles V, Concepción, October 15
1550, in Zabala et al. 2021).
Although the Spanish explorations reached Biobío, it was only in the year 1550 that Pedro de
Valdivia begins a journey with the purpose of founding a city in the Penco valley. On February 21,
1550 Valdivia reaches the Biobío River. The next day, while they were holding vigil in the Andalién Valley, they were
attacked by around 20,000 Mapuches, in what is known as “The Battle of Andalién”. How
As a result of the attack, Valdivia made the decision not to advance south but to entrench itself in a
place that the Mapuches called “Penguco” and that corresponds to the current city of Penco (Figueroa
2012). In these instances Valdivia took over a hill near the Penco Valley, where they built
a palisade, which would later be used as a barrier around the hill on which they had
settled.
“They brought large pieces of wood and poles and I ordered them to be driven into the ground and tied; and a twelve-foot ditch was to be dug.
feet wide and as many feet deep, it would have a circuit of one thousand to fifteen hundred steps, the men
“Skillful and by force of arms, it took them eight days to make a strong trench with three doors.”
(Gerónimo de Vivar in Figueroa 2012).
Valdivia related in a letter to the King:
"...it was so good that it could be defended against the French, which was done by force of arms. It was done
to give some rest to the conquerors and to store our baggage, wounded and sick, and
so that we could go out and fight when we wanted and not when the Indians wanted” (Pedro de Valdivia
in Figueroa 2012).

On March 3, 1550, the construction of the Penco fort was completed, whose main objective was the
protect themselves from Mapuche “attacks”, but which also allowed Valdivia to manage the space
distributing plots of land for the construction of temporary homes before the arrival of winter (Figueroa
2012).
The clashes between Mapuches and Spaniards continued and days later a new battle begins
outside the Penco fort built by the conquistadors. The Mapuches, led by Ainavillo,
They advanced against the fortress, surrounding it, but were unable to jump the ditch, so the attack was limited.
to throw arrows and stones. Valdivia took advantage of the situation and ordered the departure to the field of Jerónimo de
Alderete with fifty horses, who fell on the Mapuches without them having much more
what to do This attack alerted the most distant Mapuche divisions, who immediately retreated.
The battle resulted in more than a thousand Mapuche deaths and the bloody persecution of the others.
combatants who managed to escape. The Spanish took them prisoners to take as samples.
before the governor. The rest of the prisoners had their throats cut and others mutilated, and then they were put in prison.
freedom for them to return to their lands (Figueroa 2012).
Pedro de Valdivia carried out the definitive foundation of the city of “Concepción” in the current commune of
Penco on October 5, 1550 (Figure 3-5 to Figure 3-7). The name he gave to the city was
that of “The Conception
of María Purísima del Nuevo Extremo” (Figueroa 2012).

The city of Concepción, while located in Penco, experienced 4 devastating earthquakes and
tsunamis that forced it to move to its current site in the Mocha Valley, chosen as the “least
bad” of three proposed (Figueroa, 2012).
The first earthquake occurred only 20 years after its founding, in the year 1570. The earthquake was so
destructive that forced its inhabitants to completely rebuild their city. The earthquake was described by
the Spanish chronicler Góngora y Marmolejo:
“On February 8, 1570, Ash Wednesday, at around 9 in the morning, a violent earthquake
earth preceded by many thunderous underground noises overwhelmed the entire population, who
At that time, most of them, because it was a day of observance, were hearing mass in the temples. It was a
major earthquake; the first to surprise the conquerors in these lands
penquistas... and then, one after another, many giant waves rose in the sea and, coming out of
their usual limit, they began to flood the entire city” (Figueroa 2012).
The following earthquakes occurred in the years 1657 and 1730, while the last of the earthquakes
events that occurred when Concepción was still located in the current Penco occurred on May 25, 1751. Later
To this last earthquake, the authorities convened a town hall on September 25, 1751, deciding
the definitive transfer of the city of Concepción. The locations proposed for subsequent relocation
They were Landa, Loma de Parra, the Mocha Valley and Chiguayante. After reviewing the different points, and
After discarding some due to the problem of water supply, a reconnaissance of the area of the
Valle de la Mocha and it was decided to choose this site for the reconstruction of the city of Concepción
(Figueroa, 2012).

From this moment the paths of Concepción and Penco separate. The place devastated by disasters
natural areas begins to be called “ruined place of Penco” to differentiate it from the city of
Concepción in its new location. The only vestiges of the ancient city are the monastery of Las
Trinitarias, formed in 1570, and the La Planchada fort, built in 1687, which still remain, being
the latter declared a National Monument (in the category of Historical Monument) in 1977. In
Penco governed the occupation prohibition for approximately 90 years, so its abandonment was almost
total, except for about 50 families who remained there dedicating themselves to fishing or agriculture.
subsistence, as well as certain commerce. After the independence of Chile, in 1822,
The families who still lived among the ruins of the ancient city send a letter to the Supreme Director
Bernardo O'Higgins, asking him to let them live in the place freely. They don't get a response until
March 29, 1843, when the then president Manuel Bulnes granted Penco the title of Villa
(Fuentealba and Vera 2020).
DYNAMICS OF MISCELLANEOUSNESS
The establishment of the city of Concepción in Penco, as a key axis in the Hispanic conquest, led
to promptly consolidate the occupation of the territory and consequently the occupation
shared from the same space
producing various consequences. The Mapuche population suffered a
3-27
==================================================================================
PAGE 41
==================================================================================
Chapter 3 Baseline (Section C)
Cultural Heritage
EIA Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Production of
Rare Earth Concentrate
demographic decline from which it was never able to recover, leading to the conclusion that at the end of the 16th century the
The population of Concepción and Biobío would not exceed 25,000 inhabitants.
The Spanish occupation of the territory gradually intensified, especially north of the Biobío.
This caused the coexistence between Mapuche and Spaniards to develop under the dynamics
Spanish, a situation that was consolidated after the Mapuche victory in Curalaba, in 1598, as a result of the
change of the Hispanic strategy that establishes the border between Spaniards and Mapuche at the Biobío River. This
means a turning point, since the border space becomes a place of interaction,
generating cultural, economic, social exchanges and intense mixing, altering the configuration
existing until that moment. All this led to the omission or invisibility of indigenous elements
that previously marked a greater presence in this territory (Fuentealba and Vera 2020).
The initial strategy of the Spanish crown was to assimilate the Mapuche population through work. many
of the conquistadors had been rural owners of modest economic situation in Spain and
They traveled to America imbued with the mission of conquering territories for the Crown and converting the Indians to
the Catholic faith (Larrañaga 2016). They also sought fame and fortune for themselves; fame was achieved in feats
military and was recognized through titles and honors, while fortune was found in the
exploitation of gold and silver. The indigenous population was incorporated into the work force through
“encomienda”, in which the Crown, represented by the Governors, placed groups of Indians in charge
of the Spaniards with the greatest military or social prestige, who were responsible for their evangelization and
be careful. In exchange, the Crown gave them the tribute that the Indians had to pay to the King as subjects.
(Larrañaga 2016).
In Chile the regulations of the encomienda were not followed rigorously. This was a territory without
greater importance for the Crown, due to its geographical remoteness and because riches were never found
in abundance. This laxity in the rules was an implicit compensation for the conquerors, for the
rigors that it meant to reside in this territory of poverty and war. This meant that in Chile the Indians
They worked in the gold pans and on the properties of their encomenderos for almost a century
after the prohibition of this type of work was decreed. The most famous gold panner was
Quilacoya, near Concepción, which once had between 10,000 and 20,000 Mapuches
working for the conquerors (Larrañaga 2016).
In Chile, as in other borders where Spanish rule had not been able to subdue the resistance
of the indigenous people, the Spanish Crown authorized indigenous slavery during the years 1602 to 1627, without
However, according to data collected by the historian Álvaro Jara (in Larrañaga 2016), the
Slavery of Mapuches would have taken place even before the authorization of the Crown. The
Mapuches captured in battles were considered war booty and were used for jobs in the
encomienda modality, although there are also chroniclers who report on the sale of indigenous people.
In 1627 slavery was prohibited, the Hispanic-Creoles carried out a series of strategies to
“obey” such a prohibition, but that they did not mean in any way the freedom of the indigenous people, but rather
3-28
==================================================================================
PAGE 42
==================================================================================
Chapter 3 Baseline (Section C)
Cultural Heritage
EIA Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Production of
Rare Earth Concentrate
the transition to another regime of forced labor. The living conditions of former slaves did not change
radically, since other forms of forced work and deprivation of liberty persisted (Larrañaga
20
16).
As a result of the above, a displacement of Mapuches from their reductions to
the ranches and the city of Concepción. Although from the beginning the migrations were not voluntary,
as can be seen, for example, in the groups of Rere, Quinel, Hualqui and Quilacoya, since the last
decade of the 17th century, the influx of those who went to work in the
Spanish ranches, and in some cases they moved so as not to return to their lands (Fuentealba and Vera
2020).
In 1774, after the parliament of Tapihue (near Yumbel), the monarchy authorities managed to
commit the most powerful and representative chiefdoms in the world to a definitive peace agreement
Mapuche, which lasted the entire rest of the colonial period and which represented, on the one hand, the definitive commitment
of all the chiefs to refrain from carrying out new malocas against the Hispanic-Creole population, as well as
the punishment of any isolated action; and, on the other, the formal recognition by the king of the
Mapuche sovereignty between the Biobío and Toltén rivers. Once the Spanish arms were defeated and the
Independence, the Chilean State found itself in practice with a situation of undeclared war with
the Mapuches and given the impossibility of subduing them by force, he was forced, in 1825, to agree with
them a truce, again through Parliaments held in Tapihue. However, the fragile peace
achieved, and unlike the colonial administration, the Chilean State never renounced its rights over
the ultra Biobío territories, in addition to continuing to harbor a feeling of deep distrust towards
who had been allies of their former enemies and who did not miss the opportunity to rise up in
weapons against authority, as happened during the revolutions of 1851 and 1859 (Bengoa 2000).
Later, with the process of Independence of Chile, the agreements that the people had achieved are unknown.
Mapuches with the Hispanics, and the process of building the Republic begins, trying to define
identities and create a symbolic universe around Chilean nationality. One of the means to
achieving this was the so-called “Decree of Chileanness” of 1818, with which Bernardo O'Higgins intended
eliminate all race nomenclature from parish records and declare everyone as Chilean. and
Later, on March 4, 1819, he decreed that the entire indigenous population was transformed into
full citizens, thereby seeking to equalize them with the rest of the “Chileans.” How
As a consequence of all these aforementioned processes, there is an omission and an invisibility of the
Mapuche element (Fuentealba and Vera 2020).
The Mapuches were condemned to live in reduced territories, where they could only practice a
small agriculture and livestock. And with the passage of time these spaces were insufficient for a
growing and impoverishing population, which is why a part of them sought better
opportunities inserting themselves into Chilean society as day laborers on agricultural estates or labor
in industrial centers (Fuentealba and Vera 2020).

INDUSTRIALIZATION IN PENCO
In the year 1840, a port was opened for commercial activities in the town of Penco, beginning a
favorable period for industrial development in the area. To support coastal port activity,
Penco was named Villa in 1843. In neighboring Lirquén, a prosperous industry was established in 1835.
miller; At the turn of the century, beach tourism was exploited and between 1880 and 1957 it developed
the extraction of coal in several deposits in the commune, the most notable being the Lirquén Mine,
one of the first to develop mineral extraction in the so-called Biobío coal basin,
even prior to Coronel, Puchoco and Lota. In addition to these activities, the activity is also developed
mill installed in Tomé, Lirquén, Concepción and Puchacay; In addition, activities are carried out
copper smelting, coal mines, brick and tile firing ovens in Penco (Fuentealba and Vera
2020; Valenzue
the 2016).
This growth driven by industrial development led to the fact that on April 25, 1898, the
I hate the quality of the city.
During the 20th century, Penco became one of the most important industrial centers in the southern area.
thanks to the arrival of industries such as the Viña del Mar Sugar Refining Company (CRAV), the Factory
National Flat Glass Factory (FNVP or Vipla) of Lirquén (later called Vidrios Lirquén), the National Factory
de Loza (which later adopted the name “Lozapenco” and, currently, “Fanaloza”), the Company
Sudamericana de Fosfatos (COSAF) (later Muelles de Penco S.A.), the Lirquén and Cerro coal mines
Verde, the Port of Lirquén, the shoe factories, among other economic activities
secondary (Valenzuela 2016) (see Figure 3-8).
Already in 1865, Penco had a population of 1,267 people and Lirquén with about 500 inhabitants,
population that subsequently contracts due to the decline of the wheat industry and that decreases even
more due to the opening of the Panama Canal, with only 7,000 inhabitants in Penco and
Lirquén (Valenzuela 2016).

The origins of the sugar industry in Penco date back to 1886, the year in which Messrs. Teodoro
Plate (financial partner) and Oscar Mengelbier (industrial and technical partner) - both naturalized immigrants
- founded the industry under the name “South American Sugar Refinery” (see Figure 3-9 and Figure 3-10).
The Penco Refinery supplied the territory south of Talca, while the Sugar Refinery Company
of Viña del Mar (CRAV) was in charge of the territory located north of that same city, reaching
form a true oligopoly (Cerda and Puentes 2019). Some years later, and under another
administration, the company begins a stage in which it diversifies its field, opening a distillery of
alcohol, a master factory, a copper and bronze foundry, also producing boxes of bitumen and charcoal
of bone. Valenzuela (2016) points out that, as in the Saltpeter Industry with its “Pulperías”, the factory
He also installed a warehouse known as “La Fortnight.” Here workers could purchase items
basic necessities through a system of “vouchers” in the form of bills worth $1.- that the same
industry gave as payment. It should be noted that the tickets were not allowed to be used in another store. The
The ticket system was used until approximately 1901, when it changed to the banknote system.
“Notebook.”

The “Viña del Mar Sugar Refinery Company” (CRAV) is recognized for its contributions to development
Penco community. The concept of industrial paternalism is useful for
understand the actions of
certain companies, who, through the delivery of various social benefits, attempted to
solve problems arising from workforce management. Examples of this are the works
financed by CRAV, between 1926 and 1976, a period in which he founded a series of establishments
social and service facilities, such as the “Refinería Sports Center”, one of the most prestigious facilities
from the south of the country towards the middle of the 20th century, as well as the CRAV Theater. Also, between 1940 and 1970,
built housing complexes adjacent to the productive space, which currently make up the
nucleus called “CRAV Complex” and which is made up of the Desiderio Guzmán and Villa Los Population
Radales (Márquez 2014; Valenzuela 2016; Cerda and Puentes 2019). These populations respond to the
principles of hygiene, whose approaches suggested that diseases in cities were
caused by the poor living and working conditions of the workers, who lived in urban spaces
unhealthy and characterized by various moral problems, such as drunkenness,
crime or prostitution. In this way, it was proposed that, to improve the health of workers,
It was necessary to modify both their physical and social environment, requiring, among other things, a new
type of housing that would allow infections to spread more easily in the habitat to be avoided
collective, health, political and moral infections (Cerda and Puentes 2019).
Around 1976, the Penco sugar refinery had to close due to the decline in production that occurred.
as a direct consequence of the import substitution policies that the state established in
that period, with the aim of creating a national agroindustry based on beets and thus activate areas
depressed agricultural areas and provide employment to agricultural labor. As a result of this, in 1953 the
Industria Azucarera Nacional S.A. (IANSA). Its economic and social impact on the area was so profound that
motivated the expansion of the project, new plants emerging throughout the territory and the development of the
rise of beet, which would later replace sugarcane (Valenzuela 2016).
The other industry that promoted the industry in Penco was the manufacture of earthenware, becoming established as
a regional tradition. In Angol, the Serra factory is remembered; in Los Sauces and Nacimiento; and in Arauco, the
Antique ceramics. The most important, due to its technical and aesthetic quality, is Lota ceramics, today
coveted by collectors, which was produced between the 1930s and 1950s. But none

The development of archaeological works referring to the colonial occupation of Penco is scarce, according to reports
Andrade et al. (2019). Two archaeological works reported in the urban area of
Penco related to such period. The first of them was carried out by Zulema Seguel in 1967, the
which corresponded to a salvage carried out in old industrial warehouses, where bones appeared
humans, architectural remains and ecofactual evidence of different raw materials. The second was a
rescue carried out by Víctor Bustos in 2007, in the same sector, but within the framework of the construction of the
Pencopolitano High School. These works once again provided evidence of human bones, contexts
domestic and architectural remains, which the author indicates would correspond to the old church of Santo
Sunday. In 2016, the Illustrious Municipality of Penco and the University of Concepción promote the
project “Rescue of the Memory of Penco through its Architectural Heritage”, carrying out
georadar surveys, which result in the identification of more than a hundred
locations where remains of colonial structures would be located between 1.5 to 3 m deep (Barba
et al. 2016 in Andrade et al., 2019).

3.16.7.2 Archaeological evaluations within the SEIA Framework
A total of 103 project files submitted for environmental evaluation were reviewed (approved, in
qualification, rejected or not admitted for processing) located up to 20 km away from the area of the
Project, among which are port, road, real estate, mining development and
waste management, among others (see Table 3-4), of which 56 do not present baselines of
cultural heritage, either by its year of entry (prior to the entry into force of Law 19,300 and its
subsequent regulation), or
well, because they developed within previously intervened areas.
Of the 47 projects that presented information surveys of the archaeological component (lines
base of archaeological cultural heritage), only 8 reported positive results in terms of
record of archaeological finds, which are summarized in Table 3-5, along with records from sources
bibliographical information compiled within the same reports.
Previous archaeological records are concentrated around the coastal strip of Concepción Bay,
or, around the main river channels of the area (Andalién and Biobío rivers) and correspond to
shell-type formations, whether or not associated with funerary remains, as well as remains of domestic activities
(such as ceramic fragments and lithic remains, whether isolated or in the form of concentrations) and contexts
funeraries (isolated burials, or cemeteries), dated between the Archaic period and the
Historical, with a predominance of evidence associated with the Late Intermediate Period, represented by the
El Vergel Cultural Complex. Previous records are located at distances between 38 m and more than 147 km from
distance from the Project. Figure 3-13 shows the previous archaeological records located up to a
distance of 5 km from the Project works, while Figure 3-14 details the previous records
closest to the Project.
The previous archaeological records closest to the Project works correspond to the sites
“Fill 1”, reported within the framework of the EIA of the Project “Operational Continuity Management Center
Residuos Concepción”, located 38.34 m from the closest work of the present Project (road
projected) (Figure 3-14), and the record “El Cabrito 01”, recorded within the framework of a survey carried out
on the occasion of the “El Cabrito Mining Project”, subsequently entered into evaluation under the name of
“BioLantánidos Project”, located 191.5 m from the closest work (Victoria Norte Extraction Zone)
(Figure 3-14).
It should be noted that on October 18, 2023, a request for intervention through drilling wells was submitted.
survey of two archaeological sites within the framework of this Project (CMN entry N°6697-2023),
including the Project area that faced the Landfill 1 site, which was located 38 m east of the layout
prior to a projected path. However, due to the record of a stratigraphic deposit during the
surveys, the Owner decided to modify the route of the road and move it more than 150 m to the West, for
which the distance indicated now (206 m to the East) corresponds to the new layout of the road
projected.

3.16.7.3 National Monuments with Declaration
In accordance with the provisions of the “Environmental Impact Assessment Guide for National Monuments
belonging to the Cultural Heritage in the SEIA” (SEA, 2012), the search and verification of
the presence/absence of Historical Monuments within the Project Area of Influence, which
showed the absence of National Monuments with declaration inside or in the vicinity of the works,
Therefore, the search range was expanded for the characterization of nearby Historical Monuments.
to the Project area up to a radius of 20 km away.
From the review of the list of National Monuments with declaration5 located up to 20 km
away from the Project area, a total of 17 Monuments were identified in the category of
Historical Monument, located between the communes of Tomé, Penco, Talcahuano, Hualpén and Concepción
(11 of them located in the latter commune), which are located at distances between 3 km and 12.3 km
of the planned works (see Table 3-6 and Figure 3-22).

A shell was identified with human and animal bone remains, as well as lithic remains, a stove, beads
